00:55:07","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:07","title":"The Gifted Listener: Composer Aaron Copland on Honing Your Talent for Listening to Music","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=74414","description":"<h3>&#8220;There are few pleasures in art greater than the secure sense that one can recognize beauty when one comes upon it… Recognizing the beautiful in an abstract art like music partakes somewhat of a minor miracle.&#8221;</h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Music-Imagination-Charles-Norton-Lectures/dp/0674589157/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"536\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicandimagination_aaroncopland.jpg?fit=320%2C536&#38;ssl=1\" class=\"cover alignright size-medium\" alt=\"The Gifted Listener: Composer Aaron Copland on Honing Your Talent for Listening to Music\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicandimagination_aaroncopland.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicandimagination_aaroncopland.jpg?resize=320%2C536&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicandimagination_aaroncopland.jpg?resize=600%2C1006&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicandimagination_aaroncopland.jpg?resize=240%2C402&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicandimagination_aaroncopland.jpg?resize=768%2C1287&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicandimagination_aaroncopland.jpg?resize=917%2C1536&#38;ssl=1 917w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px\" /></a></p><p>&#8220;Even poetry, Sweet Patron Muse forgive me the words, is not what music is,&#8221; the poet Edna St. Vincent Millay <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/03/15/writers-on-music/\">wrote to a friend</a>, adding the requisite flamboyance of a 1920s radical: &#8220;Without music I should wish to die.&#8221;</p>\n<p>Months after Millay&#8217;s death, Harvard offered its prestigious Charles Edward Norton Professorship of Poetry for the 1951&#8211;1952 academic year to the composer <strong>Aaron Copland</strong> (November 14, 1900&#8211;December 2, 1990) &#8212; the first non-poet to hold the post since its inception a quarter century earlier. More than a decade before he received the Presidential Medal of Freedom for capturing the human experience in music, Copland brought to his six lectures, later published as <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Music-Imagination-Charles-Norton-Lectures/dp/0674589157/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>Music and Imagination</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/music-and-imagination/oclc/318456048&#038;referer=brief_results\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>public library</em></a>), not only the mind of an extraordinary musician but the central concern of his life &#8212; the artist&#8217;s role in the human family and the vital mutual nourishment between those who make art and those whose lives art touches, with a particular focus on the most commonly underappreciated agent in the musical universe: the gifted listener. </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_74408\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland_young_color.jpg?resize=680%2C1002&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"1002\" class=\"size-full wp-image-74408\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland_young_color.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland_young_color.jpg?resize=320%2C472&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland_young_color.jpg?resize=600%2C885&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland_young_color.jpg?resize=240%2C354&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland_young_color.jpg?resize=768%2C1132&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland_young_color.jpg?resize=1042%2C1536&#38;ssl=1 1042w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Aaron Copland on his way to a concert in Paris, early 1920s. (Photographer unknown. Library of Congress Archives.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Copland begins by considering the surface contrasts and deeper resonances between poetry and music:</p>\n<blockquote><p>I used to harbor a secret feeling of commiseration for poets&#8230; trying to make music with nothing but words at their command. I suppose there exist at all times some few men<a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/10/17/ursula-k-le-guin-gender/\">*</a> who have that much magic in them, but words at best will always seem to a composer a poor substitute for tones&#8230; Later&#8230; I came gradually to see that beyond the music of both arts there is an essence that joins them &#8212; an area where the meanings behind the notes and the meaning beyond the words spring from some common source&#8230; The poetry of music&#8230; signifies the largest part of our emotive life &#8212; the part that <em>sings</em>.</p></blockquote>\n<p>The poetry of music, Copland intimates, is composed both by the musician, in the creation of music and its interpretation in performance, and by the listener, in the act of listening that is itself the work of reflective interpretation. This makes listening as much a creative act as composition and performance &#8212; not a passive receptivity to the object that is music, but an active practice that confers upon the object its meaning: an art to be mastered, a talent to be honed. (In the same era, the great humanistic philosopher and psychologist Erich Fromm was making the same countercultural point about <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/10/29/the-art-of-loving-erich-fromm/\">the art of loving</a>, as distinct from the damaging cultural notion of love as an object to be found and passively received.) </p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_74423\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/music-pink-and-blue-no-2-by-georgia-okeeffe-1918_print?sku=s6-21832132p4a1v1?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicpinkandblue_georgiaokeeffe.jpg?resize=680%2C821&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"821\" class=\"size-full wp-image-74423\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicpinkandblue_georgiaokeeffe.jpg?w=2048&#38;ssl=1 2048w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicpinkandblue_georgiaokeeffe.jpg?resize=320%2C387&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicpinkandblue_georgiaokeeffe.jpg?resize=600%2C725&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicpinkandblue_georgiaokeeffe.jpg?resize=240%2C290&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicpinkandblue_georgiaokeeffe.jpg?resize=768%2C928&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicpinkandblue_georgiaokeeffe.jpg?resize=1272%2C1536&#38;ssl=1 1272w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicpinkandblue_georgiaokeeffe.jpg?resize=1695%2C2048&#38;ssl=1 1695w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/musicpinkandblue_georgiaokeeffe.jpg?w=1360&#38;ssl=1 1360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\"><em>Music, Pink and Blue No. 2</em> by Georgia O&#8217;Keeffe, 1918. (Available <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/music-pink-and-blue-no-2-by-georgia-okeeffe-1918_print?sku=s6-21832132p4a1v1?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">as a print</a> and as <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/cards?sort=new?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">stationery cards</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Copland observes that because the process by which music gives voice to our inner lives is so delicate and complex, it becomes &#8220;a very hazardous undertaking,&#8221; for there are many points at which it can break down:</p>\n<blockquote><p>At no point can you seize the musical experience and hold it. Unlike that moment in a film when a still shot suddenly immobilizes a complete scene, a single musical moment immobilized makes audible only one chord, which in itself is comparatively meaningless. This never-ending flow of music <em>forces</em> us to use our imaginations, for music is in a continual state of becoming.</p></blockquote>\n<p>This sentiment he borrows from Auden, who <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/08/10/auden-dyers-hand-reading-writing/\">thought deeply and widely about the life of art</a> and who believed that &#8220;a verbal art like poetry is reflective; it stops to think. Music is immediate; it goes on to become.&#8221; This becoming, Copland argues, is an imaginative act both for the musician and for the listener:</p>\n<blockquote><p>The more I live the life of music the more I am convinced that it is the freely imaginative mind that is at the core of all vital music making and music listening&#8230; An imaginative mind is essential to the creation of art in any medium, but it is even more essential in music precisely because music provides the broadest possible vista for the imagination since it is the freest, the most abstract, the least fettered of all the arts: no story content, no pictorial representation, no regularity of meter, no strict limitation of frame need hamper the intuitive functioning of the imaginative mind.</p></blockquote>\n<p>While elsewhere on the Harvard campus the psychologist Jerome Bruner was incubating his pioneering insight into <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/01/20/jerome-bruner-actual-minds-possible-worlds-storytelling/\">the key to great storytelling</a> and positing that creative writers both need and make creative readers, Copland writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>All musicians, creators and performers alike, think of the gifted listener as a key figure in the musical universe.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_74424\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/composition-8-by-wassily-kandinsky-1920s_print?sku=s6-21832152p4a1v1?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/kandinsky_composition8-scaled.jpg?resize=680%2C476&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"476\" class=\"size-full wp-image-74424\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/kandinsky_composition8-scaled.jpg?w=2560&#38;ssl=1 2560w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/kandinsky_composition8-scaled.jpg?resize=320%2C224&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/kandinsky_composition8-scaled.jpg?resize=600%2C420&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/kandinsky_composition8-scaled.jpg?resize=240%2C168&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/kandinsky_composition8-scaled.jpg?resize=768%2C538&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/kandinsky_composition8-scaled.jpg?resize=1536%2C1075&#38;ssl=1 1536w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/kandinsky_composition8-scaled.jpg?resize=2048%2C1434&#38;ssl=1 2048w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/kandinsky_composition8-scaled.jpg?w=1360&#38;ssl=1 1360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\"><em>Composition 8</em> by Wassily Kandinsky, 1920s, inspired by the artist&#8217;s experience of listening to a Wagner symphony. (Available <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/composition-8-by-wassily-kandinsky-1920s_print?sku=s6-21832152p4a1v1?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">as a print</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>A century after the underappreciated genius Margaret Fuller insisted that <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/12/04/margaret-fuller-on-music/\">&#8220;all truth is comprised in music and mathematics,&#8221;</a> Copland nuances the mathematical splendor of music with the shadings of its subjective reception in the listener&#8217;s mind. In a sentiment evocative of Nabokov&#8217;s famous aphorism <a href\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/01/19/stephen-jay-gould-nabokov-butterflies/\"><em>&#8220;There is no science without fancy, and no art without facts,&#8221;</em></a> Copland insists that the fancy &#8212; the creative imagination of the gifted listener &#8212; is what renders &#8220;the facts of music, so called&#8221; meaningful. He writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Listening is a talent, and like any other talent or gift, we possess it in varying degrees&#8230; There are two principal requirements for talented listening: first, the ability to open oneself up to musical experience; and secondly, the ability to evaluate critically that experience. Neither of these is possible without a certain native gift. Listening implies an inborn talent of some degree, which, again, like any other talent, can be trained and developed. This talent has a certain &#8220;purity&#8221; about it. We exercise it, so to speak, for ourselves alone; there is nothing to be gained form it in a material sense. Listening is its own reward; there are no prizes to be won, no contests of creative listening. But I hold that person fortunate who has the gift, for there are few pleasures in art greater than the secure sense that one can recognize beauty when one comes upon it&#8230; Recognizing the beautiful in an abstract art like music partakes somewhat of a minor miracle.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Echoing Goethe&#8217;s exuberant case for <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/08/28/conversations-of-goethe-eckermann-creativity/\">the perceptive powers of beginner&#8217;s mind</a>, he writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>The sensitive amateur, just because he lacks the prejudices and preconceptions of the professional musician, is sometimes a surer guide to the true quality of a piece of music. The ideal listener&#8230; would combine the preparation of the trained professional with the innocence of the intuitive amateur&#8230; The ideal listener, above all else, possesses the ability to lend himself to the power of music.</p>\n<p>[&#8230;]</p>\n<p>Without theories and without preconceived notions of what music ought to be, [the gifted listener] lends himself as a sentient human being to the power of music&#8230; We all listen on an elementary plane of musical consciousness&#8230; On that level, whatever the music may be, we experience basic reactions such as tension and release, density and transparency, a smooth or angry surface, the music&#8217;s swellings and subsidings, its pushing forward or hanging back, its  length, its speed, its thunders and whisperings &#8212; and a thousand other psychologically based reflections of our physical life of movement and gesture, and our inner, subconscious mental life.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_74419\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/job-and-his-family-restored-to-prosperity-by-william-blake-1805_print?sku=s6-21832017p4a1v1?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/04/blake_bookofjob_music_small.jpg?resize=680%2C577&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"577\" class=\"size-full wp-image-74419\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/04/blake_bookofjob_music_small.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/04/blake_bookofjob_music_small.jpg?resize=320%2C272&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/04/blake_bookofjob_music_small.jpg?resize=600%2C510&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/04/blake_bookofjob_music_small.jpg?resize=240%2C204&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2012/04/blake_bookofjob_music_small.jpg?resize=768%2C652&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">One of William Blake&#8217;s paintings for <em>The Book of Job</em>, 1806. (Available <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/job-and-his-family-restored-to-prosperity-by-william-blake-1805_print?sku=s6-21832017p4a1v1?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">as a print</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In essence, music performs an enchanted act of subconscious storytelling. (Which might be why Maurice Sendak <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2019/10/17/the-shape-of-music-maurice-sendak/\">considered musicality the key to great storytelling</a>.) In a sentiment at first blush inflammatory, especially for music-lovers and especially coming from a musician, Copland writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>The power of music to move us is something quite special as an artistic phenomenon [but] I do not hold that music has the power to move us beyond any of the other arts.</p></blockquote>\n<p>The singular power of music, as Copland conceives of it, makes me think of what it feels like to stand beneath the star-salted sky beholding the universe, with all of its immensity and intimacy &#8212; that grand cosmic silence singing with everything there is. He writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>There is something about music that keeps its distance even at the moment that it engulfs us. It is at the same time outside and away from us and inside and part of us. In one sense it dwarfs us, and in another we master it. We are led on and on, and yet in some strange way we never lose control. It is the very nature of music to give us the distillation of sentiments, the essence of experience transfused and heightened and expressed in such fashion that we may contemplate it at the same instant that we are swayed by it.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_74407\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland1.jpg?resize=680%2C703&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"703\" class=\"size-full wp-image-74407\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland1.jpg?w=828&#38;ssl=1 828w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland1.jpg?resize=320%2C331&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland1.jpg?resize=600%2C620&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland1.jpg?resize=240%2C248&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/09/aaroncopland1.jpg?resize=768%2C794&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Aaron Copland conducting in rehearsal at the Shed, Tanglewood. (Date and photographer unknown. Library of Congress Archives.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Leaning on philosopher Susanne Langer&#8217;s influential inquiry into <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/05/24/susanne-langer-music/\">what gives music its power</a> and her conclusion that &#8220;music is our myth of the inner life,&#8221; Copland returns to the gifted listener as a crucial instrument for the power of music and a crucial agent in its collaborative mythmaking:</p>\n<blockquote><p>A healthy musical curiosity and a broad musical experience sharpens the critical faculty of even the most talented amateur.</p>\n<p>[&#8230;]</p>\n<p>The dream of every musician who loves his art is to involve gifted listeners everywhere as an active force in the musical community. The attitude of each individual listener, especially the gifted listener, is the principal resource we have in bringing to fruition the immense musical potentialities of our own time.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Complement this fragment of the wholly insightful <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Music-Imagination-Charles-Norton-Lectures/dp/0674589157/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>Music and Imagination</em></strong></a> &#8212; which went on to <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/07/06/john-coltrane-creative-urge/\">inspire the young John Coltrane</a> and an entire generation of other artists &#8212; with composer Elliott Schwartz, writing a generation later, on <a href=\"\">the seven essential skills of listening</a>, then revisit Bob Dylan on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/08/27/bob-dylan-jonathan-cott/\">music as an instrument of truth</a>, Aldous Huxley on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/04/05/aldous-huxley-music-at-night/\">music as an instrument of transcendence</a>, and a tender meditation on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/08/21/clemency-burton-hill-pablo-casals-albert-schweitzer-bach/\">music and the mystery of aliveness</a>.</p>\n<hr /><h3>donating = loving</h3><p class=\"flipboard-keep\">For 15 years, I have been spending hundreds of hours and thousands of dollars each month to keep <em>Brain Pickings</em> going. It has remained free and ad-free and alive thanks to patronage from readers. I have no staff, no interns, no assistant — a thoroughly one-woman labor of love that is also my life and my livelihood. If this labor makes your life more livable in any way, please consider aiding its sustenance with <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/donate/\">donation</a>.</p>\n\n<hr />\n\n<h3>newsletter</h3>\n<p>Brain Pickings has a free weekly newsletter. 00:55:06","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:06","title":"Bridging the Island Universes of Our Experience: Aldous Huxley on Making Sense of Ourselves and Each Other","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=74359","description":"<h3>&#8220;To see ourselves as others see us is a most salutary gift. Hardly less important is the capacity to see others as they see themselves.&#8221;</h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Doors-Perception-Heaven-Hell/dp/0061729078/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"510\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/thedoorsofperception_huxley.jpg?fit=320%2C510&#38;ssl=1\" class=\"cover alignright size-medium\" alt=\"Bridging the Island Universes of Our Experience: Aldous Huxley on Making Sense of Ourselves and Each Other\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/thedoorsofperception_huxley.jpg?w=940&#38;ssl=1 940w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/thedoorsofperception_huxley.jpg?resize=320%2C510&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/thedoorsofperception_huxley.jpg?resize=600%2C957&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/thedoorsofperception_huxley.jpg?resize=240%2C383&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/thedoorsofperception_huxley.jpg?resize=768%2C1225&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px\" /></a></p><p>Conversing with a symphonic-minded physicist and a science-spirited musician on a small boat off the coast of a small island, I express my skepticism that the swell of digital records would improve posterity&#8217;s ability to know us better than we know our antecedents. A life, my companions argue as a thousand tiny waves scatter the late-summer sun into a shimmering constellation around us, is immensely easier to reconstruct from the mass of emails and text messages we will each leave behind than it is from a handful of faded letters in a Victorian desk-drawer. </p>\n<p>There is a surface logic to this reasoning. Despite my <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/12/10/emily-dickinson-love-letters-susan-gilbert/\">years-long immersion</a> in the totality of Emily Dickinson&#8217;s surviving archives, I &#8212; and you, and she &#8212; will never have a final theory of who this person, this flickering constellation of poetic conceits and personal contradictions, really was. But even as an archive-dwelling scholar frequently forestalled by the dearth of surviving records of bygone lives, I doubt that more information equals more illumination. We can hardly fathom our own depths, much less another&#8217;s &#8212; no matter the count of waves.</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_57262\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"http://oliverjeffers.com/paintings/measuring-land-and-sea\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/09/oliverjeffers_sea.jpg?resize=680%2C433&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"Painting by Oliver Jeffers from his series Measuring Land and Sea\" width=\"680\" height=\"433\" class=\"size-full wp-image-57262\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/09/oliverjeffers_sea.jpg?w=703&#38;ssl=1 703w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/09/oliverjeffers_sea.jpg?resize=240%2C153&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/09/oliverjeffers_sea.jpg?resize=320%2C204&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/09/oliverjeffers_sea.jpg?resize=600%2C382&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Painting by Oliver Jeffers from his series <a href=\"http://oliverjeffers.com/paintings/measuring-land-and-sea\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>Measuring Land and Sea</em></a></figcaption></figure>\n<p>It is less a problem of records than a problem of reckonings. We habitually see ourselves not as we are but as we aspire to be or fear we might be. Too readily, too unconsciously, we absorb who the world tells us we are, then live into &#8212; up to, or down to &#8212; that image, tender porous creatures that we are. (That, of course, is the most toxic effect of bigotry &#8212; we come to internalize our own devaluation by society, even if we consciously believe otherwise.) All the while, half-opaque as we are to ourselves, we keep trying to communicate to others what we want, what we mean, what it is like to be us. Even at their most honest and self-aware, these transmissions are irresolute and incomplete. Often, they are warped by our yearning to appear a certain way to the receiver, to achieve a certain effect with the signal &#8212; ripples on the surface of the self, catching the light depending on the position of the observer and the fleeting weather system of the observed. The Victorian love letter and the text message, the memoir and the Instagram selfie &#8212; they are all fragments of self-expression frozen in time, expressing a self fragmentary and discontinuous across the sweep of a life, fragments that can never reconstitute for posterity a complete and cohesive portrait of a person, because to be a person is to be perpetually contradictory and incomplete. </p>\n<p>There is strange consolation in this, in knowing ourselves and each other only incompletely &#8212; a mercy that saves us from the tyranny of a final verdict on who and what we are.</p>\n<p>The conversation on the boat reminded me of a passage by <strong>Aldous Huxley</strong> (July 26, 1894&#8211;November 22, 1963), exquisitely illustrative of these ambiguities and ambivalences of personhood. </p>\n<figure  class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/07/aldoushuxley_square.jpg?w=680&#038;ssl=1\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Aldous Huxley</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In his 1954 classic <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Doors-Perception-Heaven-Hell/dp/0061729078/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>The Doors of Perception</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/doors-of-perception/oclc/839429436&#038;referer=brief_results\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>public library</em></a>), which explores a particular biochemical method for plumbing those unfathomed depths of personhood far beneath the surface waves of the self, he makes this astute general observation:</p>\n<blockquote><p>Human beings are immensely complicated creatures, living simultaneously in a half dozen different worlds. Each individual is unique and, in a number of respects, unlike all the other members of the species. None of our motives is unmixed, none of our actions can be traced back to a single source and, in any group we care to study, behavior patterns that are observably similar may be the result of many constellations of dissimilar causes.</p></blockquote>\n<p>The confusion only deepens when two complexities try to make sense of each other, as we do whenever we connect with one another:</p>\n<blockquote><p>To see ourselves as others see us is a most salutary gift. Hardly less important is the capacity to see others as they see themselves.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Such clarity of vision across <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/08/17/annaka-harris-conscious/\">the abyss of subjective experience</a> is inherently challenging &#8212; we inhabit, in Huxley&#8217;s lovely poetic image, &#8220;island universes.&#8221; He writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>We live together, we act on, and react to, one another; but always and in all circumstances we are by ourselves. The martyrs go hand in hand into the arena; they are crucified alone. Embraced, the lovers desperately try to fuse their insulated ecstasies into a single self-transcendence; in vain. By its very nature every embodied spirit is doomed to suffer and enjoy in solitude. Sensations, feelings, insights, fancies &#8212; all these are private and, except through symbols and at second hand, incommunicable. We can pool information about experiences, but never the experiences themselves. From family to nation, every human group is a society of island universes.</p>\n<p>Most island universes are sufficiently like one another to permit of inferential understanding or even of mutual empathy or “feeling into.” Thus, remembering our own bereavements and humiliations, we can condole with others in analogous circumstances, can put ourselves (always, of course, in a slightly Pickwickian sense) in their places. But in certain cases communication between universes is incomplete or even nonexistent.</p></blockquote>\n<figure id=\"attachment_72737\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-from-thomas-wrights-an-original-theory-or-new-hypothesis-of-the-universe-1750_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?resize=680%2C977&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"977\" class=\"size-full wp-image-72737\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?resize=240%2C345&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?resize=320%2C460&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?resize=768%2C1103&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/02/thomaswright_galaxies3.jpg?resize=600%2C862&#38;ssl=1 600w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Art from the 1750 book <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/02/16/thomas-wright-original-theory/\"><em>An Original Theory or New Hypothesis of the Universe</em></a> by Thomas Wright, who originated the &#8220;island universes&#8221; concept. (Available as <a href=\"https://society6.com/product/art-from-thomas-wrights-an-original-theory-or-new-hypothesis-of-the-universe-1750_print?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a print</a>, as a <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-face-masks?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">face mask</a>, and as <a href=\"https://society6.com/brainpicker/collection/vintage-science-cards?curator=brainpicker\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">stationery cards</a>.)</figcaption></figure>\n<p>In certain cases, Huxley observes, those other minds appear to &#8220;belong to a different species and inhabit a radically alien universe&#8221; &#8212; none among us can be anything more than a bewildered visitor to the wonderlands which Bach and Blake called home. Even in less extreme cases, even in the everyday wonderlands we ourselves inhabit, the limitations of language &#8212; our primary instrument for outrospection &#8212; keep us from inviting others into the place where we live. Drawing on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/02/13/william-blake-paradise-lost/\">that timeless line</a> from Milton&#8217;s <em>Paradise Lost</em> &#8212; a line that might just be the most succinct summation of all philosophy and all psychology &#8212; Huxley writes:</p>\n<blockquote><p>The mind is its own place&#8230; Words are uttered, but fail to enlighten. The things and events to which the symbols refer belong to mutually exclusive realms of experience.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Complement with James Baldwin, writing in the same era, on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/05/24/james-baldwin-life-magazine-1963/\">&#8220;the doom and glory of knowing who you are and what you are&#8221;</a> and a sweeping Borges-infused reflection on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/06/10/the-mirror-of-enigmas-borges-bloy/\">chance, the universe, and the fragility of knowing who we are</a>, then revisit Huxley on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/04/05/aldous-huxley-music-at-night/\">the power of music</a> and <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2021/05/11/alxous-huxley-knowledge-understanding/\">the antidote to our existential helplessness</a>.</p>\n<hr /><h3>donating = loving</h3><p class=\"flipboard-keep\">For 15 years, I have been spending hundreds of hours and thousands of dollars each month to keep <em>Brain Pickings</em> going. It has remained free and ad-free and alive thanks to patronage from readers. I have no staff, no interns, no assistant — a thoroughly one-woman labor of love that is also my life and my livelihood. 00:55:06","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:06","title":"How (Not) to Be a Writer: Chekhov on Why the Task of Art Is Not to Solve Problems But to Formulate Questions","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=74304","description":"<h3>&#8220;Anyone who says that the artist&#8217;s sphere leaves no room for questions, but deals exclusively with answers, has never done any writing or done anything with imagery.&#8221;</h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/How-Write-Like-Chekhov-Inspiration/dp/1569242593/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"495\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/howtowritelikechekhov.jpg?fit=320%2C495&#38;ssl=1\" class=\"cover alignright size-medium\" alt=\"How (Not) to Be a Writer: Chekhov on Why the Task of Art Is Not to Solve Problems But to Formulate Questions\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/howtowritelikechekhov.jpg?w=880&#38;ssl=1 880w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/howtowritelikechekhov.jpg?resize=320%2C495&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/howtowritelikechekhov.jpg?resize=600%2C927&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/howtowritelikechekhov.jpg?resize=240%2C371&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/09/howtowritelikechekhov.jpg?resize=768%2C1187&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px\" /></a></p><p>It is a truism that the questions we ask shape the answers we find. It is, also, <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/04/21/susanne-langer-philosophy-in-a-new-key-questions-answers/\">a truth</a>. Another is that our questions &#8212; those wonderments, uncertainties, and quickenings of doubt that roil under the surface of life &#8212; are the atomic units of our creativity. Everything we make &#8212; our songs and our stories, our poems and our equations &#8212; we make to find out how the world works and what we are, to find out <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/03/27/alan-lightman-searching-for-stars-on-an-island-in-maine/\">how to live with our restless longing for absolutes in a relative universe</a>. Such questions &#8212; the questions that <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/08/19/david-whyte-sometimes/\">&#8220;can make or unmake a life,&#8221;</a> in the words of the perceptive poet David Whyte &#8212; are both the raw material and the end result of all great art; art is tasked not with solving the puzzles of being but with dissolving the false certainties of our near-life experience. </p>\n<p><strong>Anton Chekhov</strong> (January 29, 1860&#8211;July 15, 1904) was twenty-eight when he addressed this in letter to a friend, included in <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/How-Write-Like-Chekhov-Inspiration/dp/1569242593/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>How to Write Like Chekhov: Advice and Inspiration, Straight from His Own Letters and Work</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/how-to-write-like-chekhov-advice-and-inspiration-straight-from-his-own-letters-and-work/oclc/144525645&#038;referer=brief_results\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>public library</em></a>).</p>\n<figure id=\"attachment_74312\"  class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/antonchekhov_young.jpg?resize=680%2C939&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"939\" class=\"size-full wp-image-74312\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/antonchekhov_young.jpg?w=1436&#38;ssl=1 1436w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/antonchekhov_young.jpg?resize=320%2C442&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/antonchekhov_young.jpg?resize=600%2C828&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/antonchekhov_young.jpg?resize=240%2C331&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/antonchekhov_young.jpg?resize=768%2C1060&#38;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/antonchekhov_young.jpg?resize=1113%2C1536&#38;ssl=1 1113w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/antonchekhov_young.jpg?w=1360&#38;ssl=1 1360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">Anton Chekhov</figcaption></figure>\n<p>Corresponding with his friend Alexei Suvorin &#8212; a short story writer, playwright, and journalist, who went on to become the most influential newspaper publisher in the sunset hour of the Russian Empire &#8212; Chekhov, translated by Lena Lenček, writes on October 27, 1888:</p>\n<blockquote><p>I do sometimes preach heresies, but I have never, not once, gone so far as to deny that hard questions have no place in art. In conversations with my fellow writers, I always insist that it is not the job of the artist to solve narrowly specialized questions. It is bad for the artist to tackle what he<a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/10/17/ursula-k-le-guin-gender/\">*</a> does not understand. We have specialists for dealing with specialized questions; it is their job to make decisions about the peasant commune, the fate of capitalism, the evils of alcoholism, about boots, and female complaints.</p></blockquote>\n<p>A century before James Baldwin observed that the task of the artist is to <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2014/08/20/james-baldwin-the-creative-process/\">&#8220;drive to the heart of every answer and expose the question the answer hides&#8221;</a> and Susan Sontag insisted that the writer <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/12/19/susan-sontag-the-conscience-of-words/\">must guard against becoming an &#8220;opinion-machine,&#8221;</a> Chekhov argues that the work of the artist is not problem-solving &#8212; this is best left to those with aptitude suited to the problem at hand &#8212; but question-framing: </p>\n<blockquote><p>Anyone who says that the artist&#8217;s sphere leaves no room for questions, but deals exclusively with answers, has never done any writing or done anything with imagery. The artist observes, selects, guesses, and arranges; every one of these operations presupposes a question at its outset. If he has not asked himself a question at the start, he has nothing to guess and nothing to select.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Cautioning against the common conflation of two distinct concepts &#8212; &#8220;solving the problem&#8221; and &#8220;correctly formulating the problem&#8221; &#8212; he observes: </p>\n<blockquote><p>Only the latter is required of the artist. Not a single problem is resolved in <em>Anna Karenina</em> or <em>Eugene Onegin</em>, and yet the novels satisfy you completely because all the problems they raise are formulated correctly. It is the duty of the law courts to correctly formulate problems, but it is up to the members of the jury to solve them, each to his own taste.</p></blockquote>\n<p>Complement with Polish poet Wisława Szymborska&#8217;s superb Nobel Prize acceptance speech about <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2017/03/27/wislawa-szymborska-nobel-speech/\">the creative power of uncertainty</a> and David Whyte&#8217;s questioning poem <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/08/19/david-whyte-sometimes/\">&#8220;Sometimes,&#8221;</a> then revisit Chekhov on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2013/01/29/anton-chekhov-8-qualities-of-cultured-people/\">the 8 qualities of cultured people</a> and his <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/10/03/chekhov-6-rules/\">6 rules for a great story</a>.</p>\n<hr /><h3>donating = loving</h3><p class=\"flipboard-keep\">For 15 years, I have been spending hundreds of hours and thousands of dollars each month to keep <em>Brain Pickings</em> going. It has remained free and ad-free and alive thanks to patronage from readers. I have no staff, no interns, no assistant — a thoroughly one-woman labor of love that is also my life and my livelihood. 00:55:06","updatedTime":"2021-09-28 00:55:06","title":"An Illustrated Love Letter to Rivers","link":"https://www.brainpickings.org/?p=74205","description":"<h3>A painted landscape of fact and feeling along the flow of existence.</h3>\n<hr>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/What-River-Monika-Vaicenavičiene/dp/1592702791/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\"><img width=\"320\" height=\"319\" Rivers\" loading=\"lazy\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver.jpg?w=1000&#38;ssl=1 1000w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver.jpg?resize=320%2C319&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver.jpg?resize=600%2C598&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver.jpg?resize=240%2C239&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i2.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver.jpg?resize=768%2C766&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 320px) 100vw, 320px\" /></a></p><p>&#8220;There is a mystery about rivers that draws us to them, for they rise from hidden places and travel by routes that are not always tomorrow where they might be today,&#8221; Olivia Laing wrote in her stunning meditation on <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/05/16/olivia-laing-to-the-river/\">life, loss, and the wisdom of rivers</a> after she walked the River Ouse from source to sea &#8212; the River Ouse, in which Virginia Woolf slipped out of the mystery of life, having once observed that <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2015/10/05/virginia-woolf-past-present-moments-of-being/\">&#8220;the past only comes back when the present runs so smoothly that it is like the sliding surface of a deep river.&#8221;</a></p>\n<p>Rivers are the crucible of human civilization, pulsating with <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/07/09/nan-shepherd-living-mountain-water/\">the might and mystery of water</a>, their serpentine paths <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2018/06/21/pi-rivers/\">encoded with the precision of pi</a>, their ceaseless flow <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2020/03/26/crossing-brooklyn-ferry-janna-levin-walt-whitman/\">encoded in our greatest poems</a>. </p>\n<p>&#8220;Time is a river that sweeps me along, but I am the river,&#8221; Borges wrote in his <a href=\"https://www.brainpickings.org/2016/09/19/a-new-refutation-of-time-borges/\">timeless refutation of time</a>. </p>\n<p>But what is a river?</p>\n<p>That is what Lithuanian illustrator and storyteller <a href=\"http://monika.vaicenaviciene.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Monika Vaicenavičienė</a> contemplates in <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/What-River-Monika-Vaicenavičiene/dp/1592702791/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><strong><em>What Is a River?</em></strong></a> (<a href=\"https://www.worldcat.org/title/what-is-a-river/oclc/1199007318&#038;referer=brief_results\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><em>public library</em></a>) &#8212; part prose poem and part encyclopedia, exploring the many things a river is and can be, ecologically and existentially.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/What-River-Monika-Vaicenavičiene/dp/1592702791/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver20.jpg?resize=680%2C447&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"447\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-74212\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver20.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver20.jpg?resize=320%2C210&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver20.jpg?resize=600%2C395&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver20.jpg?resize=240%2C158&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i1.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver20.jpg?resize=768%2C505&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<p>The story begins on the banks of a river, with a little girl picking flowers &#8212; &#8220;every flower has a meaning&#8221; &#8212; and watching her grandmother sew. What unfolds is framed as the grandmother&#8217;s answer to the girl&#8217;s question of what a river is:</p>\n<blockquote><p>A river is a thread.<br />\nIt embroiders our wold with beautiful patterns.<br />\nIt connects people and places, past and present.<br />\nIt stitches stories together.</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/What-River-Monika-Vaicenavičiene/dp/1592702791/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" /></a></p>\n<p>The narrative weaves in the encyclopedic &#8212; geology and history, curious statistics about famous rivers &#8212; but fact and feeling remain entwined in the poetic.</p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/What-River-Monika-Vaicenavičiene/dp/1592702791/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver5.jpg?resize=680%2C346&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"346\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-74214\" srcset=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver5.jpg?w=1200&#38;ssl=1 1200w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver5.jpg?resize=320%2C163&#38;ssl=1 320w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver5.jpg?resize=600%2C306&#38;ssl=1 600w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver5.jpg?resize=240%2C122&#38;ssl=1 240w, https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver5.jpg?resize=768%2C391&#38;ssl=1 768w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 680px) 100vw, 680px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /></a></p>\n<blockquote><p>A river is a journey.</p>\n<p>A bubbling spring, a gap in a glacier, a boggy marsh, a silent lake &#8212; a river can begin anywhere.</p>\n<p>A river travels to many places: prairies and cities, dense forests and lush meadows, steppes and tundra, mountains and valleys. It travels through heat and cold. It leaps from dizzying heights, cascading down as a waterfall. It slinks lazily through marshes. Suddenly, it twists, then meanders. It creeps underground. It carves canyons out of mountains, reducing rock to sediment.</p></blockquote>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/What-River-Monika-Vaicenavičiene/dp/1592702791/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" src=\"https://i0.wp.com/www.brainpickings.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/08/whatisariver3.jpg?resize=680%2C346&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"\" width=\"680\" height=\"346\" /></a></p>\n<p>We see the river as home, called to imagine how many human lives the Nile touches in a single day along its 4,100-mile meander across Africa, or the Danube across the ten countries it traverses in Europe (my own native Bulgaria among them). </p>\n<p>We see the river as a habitat, to creatures as various as the hippo and the heron, the dragonfly and the platypus. </p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/What-River-Monika-Vaicenavičiene/dp/1592702791/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" /></a></p>\n<p>We see the river as a meeting-place, a muse, a name-source of countries and people, a sensory landscape, an emissary of deep time. </p>\n<p><a /></a></p>\n<p>Myth and fact converge into a larger reflection on the ceaseless flow of existence, linking the Ancient Greek myth of Oceanus &#8212; the great river encircling the Earth, from which the word <em>ocean</em> derives &#8212; with the ecological reality of Earth&#8217;s immense, interconnected, ancient system of water circulating through the atmosphere and pulsating through the biosphere. </p>\n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/What-River-Monika-Vaicenavičiene/dp/1592702791/?tag=braipick-20\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"><img loading=\"lazy\" It has remained free and ad-free and alive thanks to patronage from readers. I have no staff, no interns, no assistant — a thoroughly one-woman labor of love that is also my life and my livelihood. It comes out on Sundays and offers the week’s most inspiring reading. Here’s <a href=\"https://mailchi.mp/brainpickings/janna-levin-rebecca-elson-anne-lamott-james-gleick\">what to expect</a>. alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 25 Aug 2021 17:26:05
